  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d9a174f4-7a3f-423f-8d40-393d465c79ca'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer5969919244167801515">The adventures of Laura Ingalls and her family continue as they leave their little house on the prairie and travel in their covered wagon to Minnesota. They settle into a house made of sod on the banks of beautiful Plum Creek. Soon Pa builds them a sturdier house, with real glass windows and a hinged door. Laura and Mary go to school, help with the chores around the house,</span>
  <span id="freeText5969919244167801515" style="display:none">The adventures of Laura Ingalls and her family continue as they leave their little house on the prairie and travel in their covered wagon to Minnesota. They settle into a house made of sod on the banks of beautiful Plum Creek. Soon Pa builds them a sturdier house, with real glass windows and a hinged door. Laura and Mary go to school, help with the chores around the house, and fish in the creek. Pa’s fiddle lulls them all to sleep at the end of the day. But then disaster strikes—on top of a terrible blizzard, a grasshopper infestation devours their wheat crop. Now the family must work harder than ever to overcome these challenges.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_214756458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/214756458" />
    <a title="Michelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54041-michelle"><img alt="Michelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316924020p2/54041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/214756458?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle" href="/user/show/54041-michelle">Michelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780590118897" href="/book/show/1261820.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer214756458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8656713196136052465">Good grief, as an adult and as a parent, have I grown too practical to read and completely enjoy these books? <br/><br/>When Ma and Pa packed up the kiddos and left the Big Woods because there were too many people, less land and game to go around, I thought a little bit to myself, Um...Pa, did we think through this completely? Are you sure? Are we safe? But Pa is supposed to be an example of Great American Spirit. So, fine, we let this happen. There were some bumps in the road, but oh boy, we have some ma</span>
  <span id="freeText8656713196136052465" style="display:none">Good grief, as an adult and as a parent, have I grown too practical to read and completely enjoy these books? <br /><br />When Ma and Pa packed up the kiddos and left the Big Woods because there were too many people, less land and game to go around, I thought a little bit to myself, Um...Pa, did we think through this completely? Are you sure? Are we safe? But Pa is supposed to be an example of Great American Spirit. So, fine, we let this happen. There were some bumps in the road, but oh boy, we have some major battles for survival in Plum Creek. To the point where I wanted to grab Pa and yell, "Do you see? What can happen? With little research and no family to rely on, this new way of life you're trying out, riding high on the hog after that wheat crop comes through, can do to you? Because there's no wheat, Pa! Only grasshoppers. Listen to those freakin' Norwegians, will you? 'Grasshopper weather' isn't just some cutesy foreign term for, 'Gee, it's warm," it means that zillions of grasshoppers are going to take over the world!!! <br /><br />"And by the way, Caroline, Pa doesn't always know what's best, so seriously do not let him leave when it might blizzard. Because then he might get caught in it. And be outside for FOUR DAYS with nothing to eat but the children's Christmas candy."<br /><br />All that yelling aside, this is the reason (not Tolkien) that I want to live in a hill. With a charming creek outside. And I could skip around with my cow and my faithful dog Jack (and not my stick in the mud sister Mary) and hope my crazy, adventure-loving parents don't kill us all. Sorry, I'm yelling again.<br /><br />Moments I loved: Christmas. Again. It just warms my heart when they have these sweet, simple holidays but are just so truly happy to be together (and alive, cough, Charles). They go to church for one Christmas, and see an actual Christmas tree, and Laura gets a fur cape and muff that blows away Nellie Oleson's. <br />Ma keeping the family together and strong while Charles is gone, making money to make up for their lost wheat, or when blizzards come through and he's missing. Oh, Ma, you're so tough. <br />And Pa, when he says to Laura, "We must do the best we can, Laura, and not grumble. What must be done is best done cheerfully." Word, Pa. The next time somebody's bitchin' at me, I'm going to preach some Little House to them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8656713196136052465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_643361434" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/643361434" />
    <a title="Deborah Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus"><img alt="Deborah Markus" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/643361434?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Markus" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus">Deborah Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400046" href="/book/show/77769.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=trip-down-memory-lane">trip-down-memory-lane</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer643361434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16516194766650895664">It's easy to get so stuck on the subject matter of the stories Wilder tells that we fail to notice her brilliant, deceptively quiet writing. Her descriptions of scenery are gorgeous, of course; but I love the tiny sentences that tell so much, like this one when eight-year-old Mary and seven-year-old Laura are confronted by a wild herd of cattle:<br/><br/><i>Mary was too scared to move. Laura was too scared to stand still.</i><br/><br/>Or similarly simple descriptions of the girls waiting for their mother to come home:<br/><br/><i>The </i></span>
  <span id="freeText16516194766650895664" style="display:none">It's easy to get so stuck on the subject matter of the stories Wilder tells that we fail to notice her brilliant, deceptively quiet writing. Her descriptions of scenery are gorgeous, of course; but I love the tiny sentences that tell so much, like this one when eight-year-old Mary and seven-year-old Laura are confronted by a wild herd of cattle:<br /><br /><i>Mary was too scared to move. Laura was too scared to stand still.</i><br /><br />Or similarly simple descriptions of the girls waiting for their mother to come home:<br /><br /><i>The house was empty and still, with Ma gone. Ma was so quiet and gentle that she never made any noise, but now the whole house was listening for her.</i><br /><br />Wilder understood that the impersonal forces of nature are far more frightening than any imagined monsters, because nature doesn't care and so it can't be pleaded with or placated. When it destroys life, it's not being cruel or even indifferent. It simply <i>is.</i> As Laura learns when she thinks she can play safely in the creek after a strong rain:<br /><br /><i>The coldness soaked into her. This was not like wolves or cattle. The creek was not alive. It was only strong and terrible and never stopping. It would pull her down and whirl her away, rolling and tossing her like a willow branch. It would not care.</i><br /><br />Later, safe at home, Laura reflects:<br /><br /><i>The creek would go down. It would be a gentle, pleasant place to play in again. But nobody could make it do that. Nobody could make it do anything. Laura knew now that there were things stronger than anybody. But the creek had not got her. It had not made her scream and it could not make her cry.</i><br /><br />I hate it when people think that writing for children is limiting and limited. Laura Ingalls Wilder wrote for the entire world, or at least those members of the world who enjoy being captured and held willing prisoner by a story. She just happened to remember that children are an integral part of that group.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16516194766650895664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17971367929671718717"><b>The Ingalls Family versus the World</b><br/><br/>Laura and her family drove their covered wagon all the way to Minnesota to begin life anew.<br/><br/>Their new house?   <span id="freeText17971367929671718717" style="display:none"><b>The Ingalls Family versus the World</b><br /><br />Laura and her family drove their covered wagon all the way to Minnesota to begin life anew.<br /><br />Their new house? <div id="review_952518596" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/952518596" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/952518596?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400046" href="/book/show/77769.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=pioneers">pioneers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=midwest">midwest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer952518596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6015773912786592312">I was visiting relatives in Minnesota recently and was hit with a wave of nostalgia when I saw a sign for the Laura Ingalls Wilder museum in Walnut Grove. Somewhere in my mother's photo collection there is a picture of 8-year-old me, crouching by the grassy mound that was once the dugout home of Laura Ingalls and her family in the 1870s. Laura's stories from that period are told in the book, "On the Banks of Plum Creek." Coincidentally, Laura was also about 8 in the book.<br/><br/>I loved the Little House</span>
  <span id="freeText6015773912786592312" style="display:none">I was visiting relatives in Minnesota recently and was hit with a wave of nostalgia when I saw a sign for the Laura Ingalls Wilder museum in Walnut Grove. Somewhere in my mother's photo collection there is a picture of 8-year-old me, crouching by the grassy mound that was once the dugout home of Laura Ingalls and her family in the 1870s. Laura's stories from that period are told in the book, "On the Banks of Plum Creek." Coincidentally, Laura was also about 8 in the book.<br /><br />I loved the Little House books when I was a kid, and last summer I reread the first three in the series. If you were a fan of the TV show "Little House on the Prairie," several of the town characters are first introduced in the "Plum Creek" book, including mean Nellie Oleson. (Nellie in the book is just as awful as she was on the TV show -- she is always sneering at little Laura and making fun of her "country" ways. What a brat.)<br /><br />These stories have held up well over time and are still excellent children's books, with one caveat about the blatant racism toward Indians in book 2, the one named "Little House on the Prairie." In that book, the family had moved to Kansas and knowingly settled in what was still Indian Territory (a foolish move, but there's no point arguing about it nearly 150 years later). After some tense situations with local tribes, the family leaves Kansas and heads back to Minnesota, where the story picks up in "Plum Creek." First the family lives in a dugout home, and then Pa builds a sturdy wooden house. <br /><br />Thankfully there were no racist comments in "Plum Creek," just some good stories about homesteading in Minnesota, including a grasshopper plague that ruined the Ingalls' crops for several years, some intense and scary blizzards, and a prairie fire. Man, pioneering was rough! <br /><br />Like the other Ingalls books, there are also some charming stories of happy Christmases, helpful neighbors, and their loyal dog, Jack. I enjoyed "Plum Creek" so much that I plan to continue rereading the series. And the next time I'm in Minnesota, I'm going to stop by that museum.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6015773912786592312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3456142612" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3456142612" />
    <a title="HBalikov" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/929238-hbalikov"><img alt="HBalikov" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1271764879p2/929238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3456142612?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="HBalikov" class="user" itemprop="url" name="HBalikov" href="/user/show/929238-hbalikov">HBalikov</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400046" href="/book/show/77769.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3456142612" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10891156596598238051">This is book #4 in the series. It can be read on its own, but there is much to be gained by knowing what Pa and Ma and Mary and Laura and baby Carrie have experienced. For instance, the first book has them in the dense forests of Wisconsin in the last quarter of the 19th century. A lot of their extended family live nearby. In Little House on the Prairie, they leave Wisconsin and journey alone into Missouri and, finally, Kansas. <br/><br/>In this volume, having left Kansas for Minnesota, they have traded t</span>
  <span id="freeText10891156596598238051" style="display:none">This is book #4 in the series. It can be read on its own, but there is much to be gained by knowing what Pa and Ma and Mary and Laura and baby Carrie have experienced. For instance, the first book has them in the dense forests of Wisconsin in the last quarter of the 19th century. A lot of their extended family live nearby. In Little House on the Prairie, they leave Wisconsin and journey alone into Missouri and, finally, Kansas. <br /><br />In this volume, having left Kansas for Minnesota, they have traded their horses and wagon for a dugout home and acres of land on the banks of Plum Creek. <br /><br />This is another kind of “wilderness.” Not one with bears roaming the dense forest or with Indians roaming the plains. Pa has (as all pioneers should have) an enormous skill set that allows him to dig wells, trap and skin game, plant crops and build barns and houses. As one season melds into the next we see all these skills being put to use. Ma’s skills are just as important and Mary and Laura are certainly expected to help with caring for the livestock, cleaning and taking care of the baby. Ma is always busy with cooking, preserving, mending, milking, churning, creating clothes and doing Pa’s chores as well when he is away. <br /><br />Yet what comes through so clearly is that they have created a “home” and have a deep sense of “family.” And, most of all, it is a good life despite the snow storms, prairie fires, crop failures and marauding wild animals. Some refer to it as a simpler time, but it is clear that everyone is busy from morning to night and the opportunities for formal education are often too far away to be available. Church going and Sunday school augment the at home bible reading (one of the few books to travel with this family).<br /><br />Our soon-to-be six year old had no trouble understanding that Laura loved her little rag doll (her only doll) and what its loss might mean. She also wanted to discuss how special the opportunity for candy at Christmas was and how one of the children they met was allowed to be “mean” and “selfish.”<br /><br />From an adult’s point of view, the story moves along at a good pace and provides interesting details about what life on the rim of conventional civilization was like; what was important to these people; and, how Pa’s playing the fiddle at night could make a tough day infinitely better.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10891156596598238051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4061210309266024246">Decided to re-read this preparatory to visiting Walnut Grove!<br/><br/>When I was 7 or 8 this was my favorite of the series and all I remembered about it was the creek and the school and Laura's rivalry with Nellie Oleson.   <span id="freeText4061210309266024246" style="display:none">Decided to re-read this preparatory to visiting Walnut Grove!<br /><br />When I was 7 or 8 this was my favorite of the series and all I remembered about it was the creek and the school and Laura's rivalry with Nellie Oleson. Which is quite remarkable because that is only a couple of chapters, and the rest of the book - the BULK of the book - is the battle against poverty, drought, and mainly, GRASSHOPPERS. The descriptions of the grasshopper swarms are absolutely CHILLING. I literally had goosebumps every time they turned up, for pages upon pages. No offense, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26810.Andrew_Smith" title="Andrew Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Andrew Smith</a>, but I found these three trillion (an actual estimate) grasshoppers, in a swarm 1800 miles long and 110 miles wide, about three trillion times creepier and more horrific than the maneaters in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18079719.Grasshopper_Jungle" title="Grasshopper Jungle by Andrew Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Grasshopper Jungle</a>, which made me go "ha" and "ew" a lot but didn't give me CHILLS. Truth is, actually, a LOT stranger than fiction.<br /><br />As phenomenal as the story of the swarm is, what's even more chilling is that 25 years later the Rocky Mountain Locust was extinct. The last one was spotted in Canada in 1902. Scientists are still baffled as to what actually killed them. Probably it was us, changing the land. More here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.historynet.com/1874-the-year-of-the-locust.htm">http://www.historynet.com/1874-the-ye...</a></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780060544003" href="/book/show/318635.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=auto-bio-memoir">auto-bio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=tweens">tweens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1555949089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18146651113782097798">Listened with the family to the great Cherry Jones read this on cd and it is really (again) so surprisingly good. Listening in the car from Davenport, Iowa back to Chicago to finish it, I can't recall stretches of road (gulp). What I recall is Pa telling his story of snow blindness and falling into a ditch in a blizzard and sleeping in a bearskin coat for a couple days under six feet of snow and then, when the storm clears, seeing he was very close to his Plum Banks home and trudging in. Makes R</span>
  <span id="freeText18146651113782097798" style="display:none">Listened with the family to the great Cherry Jones read this on cd and it is really (again) so surprisingly good. Listening in the car from Davenport, Iowa back to Chicago to finish it, I can't recall stretches of road (gulp). What I recall is Pa telling his story of snow blindness and falling into a ditch in a blizzard and sleeping in a bearskin coat for a couple days under six feet of snow and then, when the storm clears, seeing he was very close to his Plum Banks home and trudging in. Makes RV camping as a way of engaging with the wilderness look a little tame, let's say.<br /><br />Some great and memorable scenes: the leeches dance, the terrifying swarm of grasshoppers, the incredibly intense blizzard, followed by one of those sweet Wilder Christmases with almost nothing to share but oyster stew and no presents but Pa's guitar music and his blizzard survival story. <br /><br />This is great autobiographical fiction, memoir, really, and a history of 1870's plains life for one (white!) family, a family facing nature as frightening as any Chthulu monster with some grace and music and game-playing and storytelling. A sweet, lyrical and evocative tale worth the name of classic. A great family story for a family to read or listen to. I am serious! </span>
  <a data-text-id="18146651113782097798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celeste" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celeste" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste">Celeste</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060264710" href="/book/show/1412427.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=historical">historical</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2433401393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17244392987264232698">I’m still completely engrossed in this series. For the first time in Laura’s story (not including Farmer Boy since it revolved around Almanzo instead), the show begins to deviate from the books that inspired them. Some of the characters in the book, while still present, differed greatly from their counterparts I have come to know through the show. There was one change I’m incredibly glad that the show made, and that was the substitution of hail for the plague of grasshoppers that hits the Ingall</span>
  <span id="freeText17244392987264232698" style="display:none">I’m still completely engrossed in this series. For the first time in Laura’s story (not including Farmer Boy since it revolved around Almanzo instead), the show begins to deviate from the books that inspired them. Some of the characters in the book, while still present, differed greatly from their counterparts I have come to know through the show. There was one change I’m incredibly glad that the show made, and that was the substitution of hail for the plague of grasshoppers that hits the Ingalls farm in the book. Those grasshoppers were disturbing and stomach-turning to read about, and I’m incredibly grateful that I didn’t have to see them on the show. However, that plague was morbidly interested to read about, and I couldn’t put the book down because I needed to know what happened and how the family bounced back. While I’ve enjoyed reading about Laura’s life, I’m becoming more and more thankful with every book read that I don’t live during the pioneer age. Their lives were insanely rough, and there was never an end to the hard work required to just continue squeaking by. I’ll take air conditioning and supermarkets over prairie life any day!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17244392987264232698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13638325207682700402">The Ingalls family has come to Minnesota after leaving Indian country when they learned that they had been given false information about being allowed to settle there--this chronology is a big fictionalized, since IRL they returned back to where they came from for a time before heading to Minnesota, but the basics of all of this are from their real lives.<br/><br/>They start off living in a dugout that Pa trades for, near a creek, where Mary and Laura go to a school and an actual church for the first time</span>
  <span id="freeText13638325207682700402" style="display:none">The Ingalls family has come to Minnesota after leaving Indian country when they learned that they had been given false information about being allowed to settle there--this chronology is a big fictionalized, since IRL they returned back to where they came from for a time before heading to Minnesota, but the basics of all of this are from their real lives.<br /><br />They start off living in a dugout that Pa trades for, near a creek, where Mary and Laura go to a school and an actual church for the first time; this is the book where we meet the colourful mean girl, Nellie Oleson (a composite character based on at least two girls Laura knew growing up) and the family has new, unexpected challenges.<br /><br />This is one of my favourite books in this series, and I enjoyed rereading it. It's also the first time I've read it silently without one of my daughters either by my side or lying in bed a bedtime.<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2448516549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7637417872405832299">Once again, I enjoyed seeing this historical era through the eyes of someone who lived it. Who knew a square broom as opposed to a circular one would be seen as a luxury to get excited about? And I hadn't thought about tumbleweeds catching on fire and threatening homes as they continued to roll as wheels of fire. I've heard of grasshopper clouds dimming the sun but to have it and the destruction the insects caused described was both frightening and fascinating. And of course, in the midst of it </span>
  <span id="freeText7637417872405832299" style="display:none">Once again, I enjoyed seeing this historical era through the eyes of someone who lived it. Who knew a square broom as opposed to a circular one would be seen as a luxury to get excited about? And I hadn't thought about tumbleweeds catching on fire and threatening homes as they continued to roll as wheels of fire. I've heard of grasshopper clouds dimming the sun but to have it and the destruction the insects caused described was both frightening and fascinating. And of course, in the midst of it all, the Ingalls family continued to learn, grow, and be their lovable selves. So, I enjoyed this story and look forward to the next book. :)<br /><br /><b>Content Advisory:</b><br /><br />No language or sexual content.<br /><br />Mary tells Laura she'll get "brown as an Indian" if she keeps running around without her bonnet on.<br /><br />Mention of many squished grasshoppers feeling slimy under bare feet.<br /><br />The process of cutting up a fish for dinner is described but without mention of blood.<br /><br />There is the threat of poverty, hunger, and even fire over the course of the book, but it never gets too intense and the Ingalls keep a positive, proactive attitude through it all.<br /><br />A little girl at school who does not play well with others yanks Laura to the ground by her braids when Laura suggets playing a game other than the one the little girl wants to play.<br /><br />This little girl is unkind in other ways as well including not letting Laura touch her favorite dolls and saying something disrespectful to Ma. Laura sometimes gets so mad at this girl she wants to slap her, but doesn't because she was taught never to strike another person. She does at one point however, get revenge by setting the girl up to get chased by a hermit crab.<br /><br />Laura disobeys Pa and Ma several times and experiences both natural consequences and fair punishment from her parents. Once, she even comes close to drowning with no one around to save her. (She is, of course, okay, but upset after finally getting out of the situation.) To her credit, though, she does learn her lessons and doesn't do the wrong things again.</span>

I feel that, as more the family goes west, the harder their life is and the nature is more dangerous and unpredictable. Since they live closer to the town, we get more interactions with other characters. The book made me appreciate all the amenities we take for granted. N</span>

I feel that, as more the family goes west, the harder their life is and the nature is more dangerous and unpredictable. Since they live closer to the town, we get more interactions with other characters. The book made me appreciate all the amenities we take for granted. Not sure if I'll read the next book in the series, but I'm glad I met this family.</span>

Any other favorite parts?<br /><br />E: When the grasshoppers were falling all over Laura and when they <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ate the wheat. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />D: That wasn't really a fun part though, was it? I mean, that was really bad for Laura and Pa, Ma, Mary, and Carrie, right?<br /><br />E: BUT DAD! The person that reads the book. It's not really happening any more. We're just hearing about it. I wasn't <i>crying</i> during that part. I was just sitting on the floor, happy, smiling, listening.<br /><br />D: Good point. Good point.<br /><br />E: Why is it a good point?<br /><br />D: It just is. Anything else?<br /><br />E: Ummmm... Nnnn... (thinking) When... AHH! When it had "The Day of Games!"<br /><br />D: Why did you like that?<br /><br />E: BECAUSE! It had a lot of PLAYING in it.<br /><br />D: Yeah. Do you think other people should read the book?<br /><br />E: YES! YES! And I liked it when Laura and Carrie were drawing with their thimbles.<br /><br />D: Me too. Can I tell them about the video?<br /><br />E: Yeah.<br /><br />D: We made a video of Eleanor saying all the chapters of On the Banks of Plum Creek. It's kindof long, but <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GWwCX-GiSgM&amp;feature=youtu.be" rel="nofollow noopener">we wanted to share it</a>.<br /><br />*EDIT* We forgot to put the stars... the MOST IMPORTANT PART OF THE REVIEW!!!! (or so says, Eleanor...)<br /><br />D: Eleanor, how many stars should we give it?<br /><br />E: Fiiiiiiiivvvvvveeeeeee!!! Five--iiiive-iveive. Five-iiiive-iveive.<br /><br />D: Got it. Really, I do.</span>

We also meet Nellie Olsen and Willie Olsen - they are really obnoxious. It was funny I thought when Nellie ended up covered in leeches. </span>

<div id="review_794747876" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794747876" />
    <a title="wanderer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6155442-wanderer"><img alt="wanderer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386166906p2/6155442.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400046" href="/book/show/77769.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6155442-wanderer?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6155442-wanderer?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6155442-wanderer?shelf=got-me-through-childhood">got-me-through-childhood</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer794747876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16622965792168215303">This isn't my favorite Laura book but it contains two of the most impressive, and perhaps famous, scenes: Nellie Olson dancing about with leeches on her legs (the absolute best example of "what goes around, comes around" I've ever seen) and the coming of the grasshoppers (nightmare material, that.) <br/><br/>This is also the book where the doll Charlotte goes and comes back--in two favorite, love-hate scenes--and the book where Laura gets a fur muff. Oh, how I wanted a muff. There are more simple, beautif</span>
  <span id="freeText16622965792168215303" style="display:none">This isn't my favorite Laura book but it contains two of the most impressive, and perhaps famous, scenes: Nellie Olson dancing about with leeches on her legs (the absolute best example of "what goes around, comes around" I've ever seen) and the coming of the grasshoppers (nightmare material, that.) <br /><br />This is also the book where the doll Charlotte goes and comes back--in two favorite, love-hate scenes--and the book where Laura gets a fur muff. Oh, how I wanted a muff. There are more simple, beautiful Christmases, of course. <br /><br />The dugout on Plum Creek might have been the coolest <i>Little House</i>, of all, and this book might have the best ending, too. <br /><br />Pa speaking: <br /><br /><blockquote>"Look, Caroline," he said, "see how Laura's eyes are shining."</blockquote><br /><br /><br />***<br /><br />Following is my review of the series, in general:<br /><br />The <i>Little House</i> books were almost as much a part of my childhood as my little sisters and my Siamese cat, and random snippets of text or an occasional illustration still pops into my mind at random moments. I’ll never hear about or witness a butchering day without remembering the drawing of Laura, playing with the pig’s bladder balloon. Most of my hazy knowledge of maple syrup making comes from <i>Little House in the Big Woods</i>. Leeches? I’m straight back with Nellie Olson <i>On the Banks of Plum Creek</i>.<br /><br />Now that I’m older, I realize how effortlessly Laura Ingalls Wilder handed young readers a slice of history. We had no clue we were learning, we just devoured the story. I can’t thank Wilder enough for that.<br /><br />The fact that many of Laura’s homes were familiar places only added to their charm. I was born (and mostly raised) in Kansas, which Laura traveled through in <i>Little House on the Prairie</i>. My mom spent her first six years in South Dakota, very near the site of the last five <i>Little House</i> books. When I was twelve we moved to Wisconsin, land of <i>Little House in the Big Woods</i>. It seems fitting, then, that as an adult I moved to Missouri, where Laura lived when she wrote the set.<br /><br />My favorite book was probably always <i>Little Town on the Prairie</i> and my least favorite was definitely <i>The Long Winter</i>. I haven’t read the books for years—they’re on my to-read-soon list—and I’m curious to know what I’ll think of them as an adult. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16622965792168215303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169931621" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169931621" />
    <a title="Dawn Trlak-Donahue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1732822-dawn-trlak-donahue"><img alt="Dawn Trlak-Donahue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242187615p2/1732822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169931621?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn Trlak-Donahue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn Trlak-Donahue" href="/user/show/1732822-dawn-trlak-donahue">Dawn Trlak-Donahue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1732822-dawn-trlak-donahue?shelf=children-non-fiction">children-non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1732822-dawn-trlak-donahue?shelf=children-classics">children-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169931621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4994815436193217303">Starting to really agree with a review I read of the Little House books. One woman wrote about how on top of things Alamonzo's family seemed in Farmer Boy. They had a permanent home, savings, etc. Whereas Papa Ingalls was a hot mess. He dragged the family away from their relatives in Wisconsin where they had a home, to Indian country. Along the way they were almost swept away down a river when he insisted they could cross it, wagon and all. (Jack, the dog, gets the short end of the stick overall</span>
  <span id="freeText4994815436193217303" style="display:none">Starting to really agree with a review I read of the Little House books. One woman wrote about how on top of things Alamonzo's family seemed in Farmer Boy. They had a permanent home, savings, etc. Whereas Papa Ingalls was a hot mess. He dragged the family away from their relatives in Wisconsin where they had a home, to Indian country. Along the way they were almost swept away down a river when he insisted they could cross it, wagon and all. (Jack, the dog, gets the short end of the stick overall. Everytime they move, he has to run under the wagon, hundreds, sometimes thousands of miles for months on end.) Then, the government made them move out of Indian country, which was just as well, seeing as the Indians were always stealing their food and tobacco. Not to mention the problems with bears and other wild animals. So, they move to Minnesota and live in a sod home for a while. When, finally, Papa builds a home on credit, their wheat gets eaten up by the locusts for 2 seasons and he has to walk 200-300 miles away each year to get work laboring on other farms in order to keep the family alive. Then, there was the time he had to live in what amounted to a snow igloo when he insisted he could go to town during winter blizzard season. He had to eat all of the measly crackers and Christmas candy to stay alive. The family had no presents that year. If I had read just the Ingalls' story, I would think that was just how everyone lived at that time. But, Alamonzo's family didn't live like that. I think it had a lot to do with crazy Papa and his wild ways.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4994815436193217303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400046" href="/book/show/77769.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer995406346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5953222057798490485">I loved reading this book while camping. I wasn't exactly in a prairie, but it was great to read this outside lost in the nature. This book was so sweet and charming, and its simplicity was refreshing after some other heavier books I was reading. I loved following Laura and Mary around their underground house, picking up plums and playing in the creek. I loved feeling happy for them when they made a button garland for Carrie's Christmas, or when they got a new cow. And I could sympathize and fee</span>
  <span id="freeText5953222057798490485" style="display:none">I loved reading this book while camping. I wasn't exactly in a prairie, but it was great to read this outside lost in the nature. This book was so sweet and charming, and its simplicity was refreshing after some other heavier books I was reading. I loved following Laura and Mary around their underground house, picking up plums and playing in the creek. I loved feeling happy for them when they made a button garland for Carrie's Christmas, or when they got a new cow. And I could sympathize and feel bad for them when their crop was destroyed by grasshopers, and Pa had to leave for many months to find work. It was such an easy, lovely book to follow and I put it down with a happy sigh of contentment when I finished. Classics like that are not to be missed; there is a special feel to the <i>Little House</i> books that is unique to them, and everyone should experience it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5953222057798490485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2694499121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2711870344470343958">This has been the most exciting yet of the series with even more disasters and fewer descriptions. It is also the volume where things start to go a bit wrong, which does make the whole story more realistic. The narrator is, as in the other books able to draw us into the tale and help us to forgive Pa (who seems to make some very unwise decisions). I don't know who I would recommend this to as it depicts a very different world from that of today's children and is not necessarily what adults would</span>
  <span id="freeText2711870344470343958" style="display:none">This has been the most exciting yet of the series with even more disasters and fewer descriptions. It is also the volume where things start to go a bit wrong, which does make the whole story more realistic. The narrator is, as in the other books able to draw us into the tale and help us to forgive Pa (who seems to make some very unwise decisions). I don't know who I would recommend this to as it depicts a very different world from that of today's children and is not necessarily what adults would want to read! But I enjoyed it enough to plough on with the next one!</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/308848683" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/308848683?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Audrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Audrey" href="/user/show/5436039-audrey">Audrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060581831" href="/book/show/1261817.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=pioneers-settlers">pioneers-settlers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=juvenile">juvenile</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=award-and-honor-recipients">award-and-honor-recipients</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=read-as-a-child">read-as-a-child</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=1870s">1870s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=favorite-heroines">favorite-heroines</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=nature">nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5436039-audrey?shelf=inspired-by-true-story">inspired-by-true-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer308848683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3569643987700335117">I loved this book! I am currently rereading the entire Little House series, which I have not visited since I was a kid. I feel like the books just keep getting better. Since it had been such a long time since I read this, I had forgotten most of the plot and, consequently, it was almost like reading this for the first time. <br/><br/>I was left with a few unanswered questions. For example:<a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ What did they eat when Pa was away the first time? They had no money and all their crops were destroye</span></span>
  <span id="freeText3569643987700335117" style="display:none">I loved this book! I am currently rereading the entire Little House series, which I have not visited since I was a kid. I feel like the books just keep getting better. Since it had been such a long time since I read this, I had forgotten most of the plot and, consequently, it was almost like reading this for the first time. <br /><br />I was left with a few unanswered questions. For example:<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ What did they eat when Pa was away the first time? They had no money and all their crops were destroyed. What were the fireball things that came down from the stovepipe? Where did the grasshoppers come from and how did they know when to leave?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The girls are growing up in this, and Laura especially tests her boundaries a bit more than in the previous books. Sometimes Mary and Laura are downright naughty, but I think that makes the stories more realistic...and of course they usually come to see the consequences of their actions. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I thought that was a mean trick of Laura to play on Nellie (regarding the bloodsuckers), even if Nellie was mean and spoiled.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I loved the attention to detail (everything from Ma opening a letter with a hairpin to the joy accompanying a long-anticipated rain), the description, and emotion that just sweeps you into this family's life in such a personal way. I feel like Wilder is really an excellent writer, and her style really makes me feel like I am there on the prairie with the Ingallses. I also love how their pioneer spirit really shines through. In the midst of so many ups and downs they always find a way to keep up hope and to keep moving forward. It's inspiring!<br /><br />{Listened on audio book narrated by Cherry Jones.}</span>
  <a data-text-id="3569643987700335117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_892358411" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/892358411" />
    <a title="Mimi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4474787-mimi"><img alt="Mimi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599529633p2/4474787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mimi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mimi" href="/user/show/4474787-mimi">Mimi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/1937339.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4474787-mimi?shelf=a-treasury-of-childhood">a-treasury-of-childhood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4474787-mimi?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer892358411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3495085999422706074">Ha! I couldn't even tell you what the cover illustration was on this one (although I'm sure it was the above) as it has been gone for so very long. <br/>A few years ago, my Book Club read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/240373.The_Children_s_Blizzard_' title='The Children's Blizzard  by David Laskin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Children's Blizzard</a>, which talked about the settling of the Midwest by the mostly Scandinavian immigrants and how harsh the land was, and really unsuitable for homesteading. While at the time, I thought of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter__Little_House___6_' title='The Long Winter (Little House, #6) by Laura Ingalls Wilder' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Long Winter</a>this book illustrates the point as well. When they first arrive, Mr. Nelson is heading west </span>
  <span id="freeText3495085999422706074" style="display:none">Ha! I couldn't even tell you what the cover illustration was on this one (although I'm sure it was the above) as it has been gone for so very long. <br />A few years ago, my Book Club read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/240373.The_Children_s_Blizzard_" title="The Children's Blizzard  by David Laskin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Children's Blizzard</a>, which talked about the settling of the Midwest by the mostly Scandinavian immigrants and how harsh the land was, and really unsuitable for homesteading. While at the time, I thought of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter__Little_House___6_" title="The Long Winter (Little House, #6) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">The Long Winter</a>this book illustrates the point as well. When they first arrive, Mr. Nelson is heading west and trades his dugout and land for their ponies and in a moment of shocking hubris, Pa wonders why he planted so few crops. <br />So many of the scenes in this book were familiar, and favorites - going to school (oh how I wanted a slate and a speller), the rushing river, the dugout itself (and I was surprised to discover I'd forgotten that they'd moved out of the dugout into a house, all bought on credit), Nellie's party and later comeuppance, and Charlotte. <br />I was also interested at how less Puritanical Ma and Pa were in this novel, how harsh the landscape was, and Pa's hiding his laughter when Laura told him they weren't jumping off the haystack, but rolling down the side. <br />A wonderful re-read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3495085999422706074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8084339-alana?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8084339-alana?shelf=educational">educational</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8084339-alana?shelf=books-to-film">books-to-film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8084339-alana?shelf=newberry-honor">newberry-honor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer289929644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8432464075583311909">This one has some of the most memorable moments in the Little House series: the little dugout house, the cow putting his foot through the roof, leeches in the creek, locusts, doing chores by following a rope between the house and barn in a blizzard..... all those moments of life in a pioneering era of hardship and pure heart. Despite knowing how these stories turn out, I still get anxious for Pa coming home in the storm, or when trying to put out a prairie fire, and get grossed out by the leeche</span>
  <span id="freeText8432464075583311909" style="display:none">This one has some of the most memorable moments in the Little House series: the little dugout house, the cow putting his foot through the roof, leeches in the creek, locusts, doing chores by following a rope between the house and barn in a blizzard..... all those moments of life in a pioneering era of hardship and pure heart. Despite knowing how these stories turn out, I still get anxious for Pa coming home in the storm, or when trying to put out a prairie fire, and get grossed out by the leeches, cheer for Laura in her troubles with Nellie Oleson, and long to see the beautiful sunsets and sunrises described. Wilder tells stories with such remarkable narrative ability, you can't help but get deeply buried into each story. I look forward to reading these over and over again.<br />5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="8432464075583311909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1702430325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5534119563186001131">Aside from Pa's failures to produce the promised wheat crops (he sure talks a helluva game about this in this book) and his need to "walk 300 miles" to find a job, this book features the Ingalls family staying in one place the entire book. <br/><br/>No worries, though. We know how irritating the Norwegians are, how the church folks can't sing in tune, and how the grasshoppers are the root of all evil. Also, Laura is a vindictive little bitch toward one of her classmates and it was kind of fantastic to rea</span>
  <span id="freeText5534119563186001131" style="display:none">Aside from Pa's failures to produce the promised wheat crops (he sure talks a helluva game about this in this book) and his need to "walk 300 miles" to find a job, this book features the Ingalls family staying in one place the entire book. <br /><br />No worries, though. We know how irritating the Norwegians are, how the church folks can't sing in tune, and how the grasshoppers are the root of all evil. Also, Laura is a vindictive little bitch toward one of her classmates and it was kind of fantastic to read. <br /><br /></span>

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('C7Q8t977GVagCXb6yXzFDwNZ2hC2QJ64el9DyLeU7WtTKoq9RPcfIqaXnCaACehhsY+l7dlAY25AbBn98ZrZog==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l6KA7jXkk8w2vASZ7v/54gaYej60ASldu1sCj94rSrvPPDbkr+iVuDAi7kWnitSMtE4Fw9sB1IuBaFi6mCV+cg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7964769188552073413">Ingalls wrote a series of historical fiction books for children based on her childhood growing up in a pioneer family. She also wrote a regular newspaper column and kept a diary as an adult moving from South Dakota to Missouri, the latter of which has been published as a book.</span>

She shares the cabin with her Pa, her Ma, her sisters Mary and Carrie, and their lovable dog, Jack.\n\nPioneer life isn’t easy for the Ingalls family, since they must grow or catch all their own food as they get ready for the cold winter. But they make the best of every tough situation. They celebrate Christmas with homemade toys and treats, do their spring planting, bring in the harvest in the fall, and make their first trip into town. And every night, safe and warm in their little house, the sound of Pa’s fiddle lulls Laura and her sisters into sleep.\n\nThe nine books in the timeless Little House series tell the story of Laura’s real childhood as an American pioneer, and are cherished by readers of all generations. They offer a unique glimpse into life on the American frontier, and tell the heartwarming, unforgettable story of a loving family.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10801497295987366171\" href=\"#\" This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
